        </div><div class="casAbstract">Understanding the correlation between the desired morphol. of nanostructures and its electrochem. properties is a prerequisite for widespread application of advanced energy materials.  Herein, 2 types of tetragonal α-Fe2O3 single crystals with a mean size of ∼200 nm, including cubic and thorhombic shapes, have been synthesized via a facile hydrothermal approach.  The as-obtained shape of α-Fe2O3 nanocrystals depends on the addn. of the metal ions precursor, the Zn2+ ions result in the cubic shape and the Cu2+ ions result in the thorhombic shape, resp.  These 2 different tetragonal α-Fe2O3 single crystals are used as anode materials for Li ion batteries (LIBs), and cubic α-Fe2O3 single crystal exhibits a better performance than thorhombic α-Fe2O3 single crystal.  The discharge capacity of cubic α-Fe2O3 single crystal is up to 1028 mA-h/g, and the c.d. is up to 1000 mA/g (1C) after 222 cycles.  Clearly, the α-Fe2O3 single crystal with controlled shapes would improve the electrochem. performance of LIBs as superior anode materials, and this approach could pave a way to develop high performance LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrKslu_LxPjzbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liDYN1b82S_xw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXltlKmur0%253D&amp;md5=a6ce93ab309330b8a59f5b4656063771</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1038/srep05753"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xie, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">CuO Single Crystal with Exposed {001} Facets-A Highly Efficient Material for Gas Sensing and Li-Ion Battery Applications</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Sci.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Single-Crystalline Bilayered V2O5 Nanobelts for High-Capacity Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Dawei; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11218-11226</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Single-cryst. bilayered V oxide nanobelts were synthesized by a simple solvothermal method.  FE-SEM and AFM analyses identified the nanobelt morphol. of the as-prepd. V oxide with a rectangular cross-section and a thickness of ∼50 nm.  XRD and TEM characterizations revealed a large (001) interlayer spacing (∼11.53 Å), which can accommodate Na-ion insertion and extn.  When applied as cathode materials in Na-ion batteries, V oxide nanobelts exhibited a high capacity of 231.4 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 80 mA g-1.  This corresponds to the theor. capacity to form Na2V2O5 on Na-ion insertion.  V oxide nanobelts also demonstrated an excellent high-rate performance and a satisfactory cyclability.  These superior electrochem. performances could be ascribed to the unique bilayered V oxide nanobelts with dominantly exposed {100} crystal planes, which provide large interlayer spacing for facile Na-ion insertion/extn.  Single-cryst. bilayered V oxide nanobelts could be promising cathode materials for high-performance Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr9Zt4QF5KMurVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liDYN1b82S_xw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslGgsLjF&amp;md5=0a89c6f143902090fa42787be2f1e390</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1039/c4ta01751j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical Orthorhombic V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> Hollow Nanospheres as High Performance Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hierarchical orthorhombic V2O5 hollow nanospheres as high performance cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, D. W.; Dou, S. X.; Wang, G. X.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Vanadyl ethylene glycolate hollow hierarchical nanospheres were synthesized via a template-free polyol-induced solvothermal process.  After sintering, vanadium pentoxide (V2O5) with well-preserved spherical structures was obtained.  Refined x-ray diffraction and TEM analyses identified that the V2O5 hollow nanospheres were constructed from hierarchical nanocrystals with predominantly exposed {110} crystal planes.  When applied as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries), the V2O5 hollow nanospheres delivered a specific discharge capacity of ∼150 mA-h/g, which is one Na+ ion insertion per V2O5 formula unit.  Theor. modeling on the vol. expansion and stress evolution on Na+ ion insertion revealed that the prolonged cycling stability could be ascribed to the porous hollow spherical architecture.  Also, the exposed {110} facets of V2O5 nanocrystals with two-dimensional diffusion paths for Na+ ion intercalation also contribute to high rate capacity and excellent cycling performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq-uydBqQ039LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgahomB0RSi6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtFSqsrjF&amp;md5=14544212d1192c43e9d57df978173edc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b07547"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ma, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Che, R.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Inheritance of Crystallographic Orientation during Lithiation/Delithiation Processes of Single-Crystal α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> Nanocubes in Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Inheritance of Crystallographic Orientation during Lithiation/Delithiation Processes of Single-Crystal α-Fe2O3 Nanocubes in Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ma, Xiaowei; Zhang, Manyu; Liang, Chongyun; Li, Yuesheng; Wu, Jingjing; Che, Renchao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Fe oxides are very promising anode materials based on conversion reactions for Li-ion batteries (LIBs).  During conversion processes, the crystal structure and compn. of the electrode material are drastically changed.  Surprisingly, inheritance of a crystallog. orientation was found during lithiation/delithiation processes of single-crystal α-Fe2O3 nanocubes by ex situ TEM.  Single-crystal α-Fe2O3 was 1st transformed into numerous Fe nanograins embedded in a Li2O matrix, and then the conversion between Fe and FeO nanograins became the main reversible electrochem. reaction for energy storage.  These Fe/FeO nanograins had almost the same crystallog. orientation, indicating that the lithiated/delithiated products can inherit the crystallog. orientation of single-crystal α-Fe2O3.  This finding is important for understanding the detailed electrochem. conversion processes of Fe oxides, and this feature may also exist during lithiation/delithiation processes of other transition-metal oxides.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoVbdA4fBwFg7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgahomB0RSi6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhs1ChtrrN&amp;md5=445590177fff5f17a5693d7051277d03</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevLett.97.187401"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ferrari, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene is the 2-dimensional building block for C allotropes of every other dimensionality.  Its electronic structure is captured in its Raman spectrum that clearly evolves with the no. of layers.  The D peak 2nd order changes in shape, width, and position for an increasing no. of layers, reflecting the change in the electron bands via a double resonant Raman process.  The G peak slightly down-shifts.  This allows unambiguous, high-throughput, nondestructive identification of graphene layers, which is critically lacking in this emerging research area.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo35CDycv0LILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgahomB0RSi6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XhtFKqtbvP&amp;md5=8b1d9f77f616aea008d55ba4fbb3f0bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1021/la804216z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Paredes, J.                 10.1021/la804216z</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D25%26publication_year%3D2009%26pages%3D5957-5968%26journal%3DLangmuir%26author%3DJ.%2BI.%2BParedes%26author%3DS.%2BVillar-Rodil%26author%3DP.%2BSol%25C3%25ADs-Fern%25C3%25A1ndez%26author%3DA.%2BMart%25C3%25ADnez-Alonso%26author%3DJ.%2BM.%2BD.%2BTasc%25C3%25B3n%26title%3DAtomic%2BForce%2Band%2BScanning%2BTunneling%2BMicroscopy%2BImaging%2Bof%2BGraphene%2BNanosheets%2BDerived%2Bfrom%2BGraphite%2BOxide%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fla804216z&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b04407&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fla804216z&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Atomic Force and Scanning Tunneling Microscopy Imaging of Graphene Nanosheets Derived from Graphite Oxide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Paredes, J. I.; Villar-Rodil, S.; Solis-Fernandez, P.; Martinez-Alonso, A.; Tascon, J. M. D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Langmuir</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">25</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5957-5968</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">LANGD5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0743-7463</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene nanosheets produced in the form of stable aq. dispersions by chem. redn. of graphene oxide and deposited onto graphite substrates have been investigated by at. force and scanning tunneling microscopy (AFM/STM).  The chem. reduced graphene oxide nanosheets were hardly distinguishable from their unreduced counterparts in the topog. AFM images.  However, they could be readily discriminated through phase imaging in the attractive regime of tapping-mode AFM, probably because of differences in hydrophilicity arising from their distinct oxygen contents.  The chem. reduced nanosheets displayed a smoothly undulated, globular morphol. on the nanometer scale, with typical vertical variations in the subnanometer range and lateral feature sizes of ∼5-10 nm.  Such morphol. was attributed to be the result of significant structural disorder in the carbon skeleton, which originates during the strong oxidn. that leads to graphene oxide and remains after chem. redn.  Direct evidence of structural disorder was provided by at.-scale STM imaging, which revealed an absence of long-range periodicity in the graphene nanosheets.  Only structured domains a few nanometers large were obsd. instead.  Likewise, the nanosheet edges appeared atomically rough and ill-defined, though smooth on the nanometer scale.  The unreduced graphene oxide nanosheets could only be imaged by STM at very low tunneling currents (∼1 pA), being visualized in some cases with inverted contrast relative to the graphite substrate, a result that was attributed to their extremely low cond.  Complementary characterization of the unreduced and chem. reduced nanosheets was carried out by thermogravimetric anal. as well as UV-visible absorption and X-ray photoelectron and Raman spectroscopies.  In particular, the somewhat puzzling Raman results were interpreted to be the result of an amorphous character of the graphene oxide material.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqP3DAIGzG4DLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhAoqvyJd8RWw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXjvF2gtrY%253D&amp;md5=003680ec80967b1e6606a3bd8e35de3c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2007.02.034"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stankovich, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dikin, D. A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Piner, R. D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kohlhaas, K. A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kleinhammes, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jia, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nguyen, S. T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ruoff, R. S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of Graphene-Based Nanosheets via Chemical Reduction of Exfoliated Graphite Oxide</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Carbon</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2007</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">45</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1558</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1565</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.carbon.2007.02.034</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D45%26publication_year%3D2007%26pages%3D1558-1565%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DS.%2BStankovich%26author%3DD.%2BA.%2BDikin%26author%3DR.%2BD.%2BPiner%26author%3DK.%2BA.%2BKohlhaas%26author%3DA.%2BKleinhammes%26author%3DY.%2BJia%26author%3DY.%2BWu%26author%3DS.%2BT.%2BNguyen%26author%3DR.%2BS.%2BRuoff%26title%3DSynthesis%2Bof%2BGraphene-Based%2BNanosheets%2Bvia%2BChemical%2BReduction%2Bof%2BExfoliated%2BGraphite%2BOxide%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.carbon.2007.02.034&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b04407&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2007.02.034&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of graphene-based nanosheets via chemical reduction of exfoliated graphite oxide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stankovich, Sasha; Dikin, Dmitriy A.; Piner, Richard D.; Kohlhaas, Kevin A.; Kleinhammes, Alfred; Jia, Yuanyuan; Wu, Yue; Nguyen, SonBinh T.; Ruoff, Rodney S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2007</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">45</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1558-1565</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Redn. of a colloidal suspension of exfoliated graphene oxide sheets in water with hydrazine hydrate results in their aggregation and subsequent formation of a high-surface-area carbon material which consists of thin graphene-based sheets.  The reduced material was characterized by elemental anal., thermogravimetric anal., SEM, XPS, NMR spectroscopy, Raman spectroscopy, and elec. cond. measurements.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrrhZ_RTUhlnLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhAoqvyJd8RWw"> &gt;&gt; The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrZwDymuubZm7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgE0rdPEaVqXg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhsVGktL%252FJ&amp;md5=5035bfda7631ad9d075e8836e61146ef</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1038/nchem.2085"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Larcher, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J. Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7saEWg_hysA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Palomares, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Serras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Villaluenga, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hueso, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7saEWg_hysA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40811f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hong, S.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHBS0wIkomorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li7saEWg_hysA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVWmtL%252FE&amp;md5=023136cc0e5ed94e18ce0d526a0000a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. techniques have been used to study the reversible insertion of sodium into hard-carbon host structures at room temp.  In this paper we compare these results with those for lithium insertion in the same materials and demonstrate the presence of similar alkali metal insertion mechanisms in both cases.  Despite the gravimetric capacities being lower for sodium than lithium insertion, we have achieved a reversible sodium capacity of 300 mAh/g, close to that for lithium insertion in graphitic carbon anode materials.  Such materials may therefore be useful as anodes in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpm125w9mEFSbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li59inb0WZAoQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXisVygtL0%253D&amp;md5=7480ac0a7bc2620ce565b1c21f1e2463</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1039/c4cp00826j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tokiwa, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Negative Electrodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Phys.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have emerged in recent years, due to their advantages of natural abundance, low cost and environmental friendliness.  In this article, we review the up-to-date research progress on anode materials for Na-ion batteries from five respects: carbon-based materials, alloy-based materials, metal oxides and sulfides based on conversion reaction, titanium-based compds. with insertion mechanism, and org. composites.  In particular, we not only summarize the Na-storage mechanism of these anodes, but also discuss the failure mechanism.  The problems and challenges assocd. with these anodes are pointed out.  Furthermore, on the basis of extensive reports and our exptl. studies, feasible strategies are suggested for designing high performance anode materials.  After further in-depth exploration and investigation, we believe that Na-ion batteries are promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries for low cost and large-scale energy storage systems in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrB4Pf2vZ5QrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li59inb0WZAoQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtFelsrnN&amp;md5=8311789a2dc4e61dabff177245235c3e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1021/acs.accounts.5b00482"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shen, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bommier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Acc.                         rightTab-cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-Ion Battery Anodes: Materials and Electrochemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Shen, Fei; Bommier, Clement; Zhu, Hongli; Ji, Xiulei; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Accounts of Chemical Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">231-240</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intermittent nature of renewable energy sources, such as solar and wind, calls for sustainable elec. energy storage (EES) technologies for stationary applications.  Li will be simply too rare for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) to be used for large-scale storage purposes.  In contrast, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are highly promising to meet the demand of grid-level storage because Na is truly earth abundant and ubiquitous around the globe.  Furthermore, NIBs share a similar rocking-chair operation mechanism with LIBs, which potentially provides high reversibility and long cycling life.  It would be most efficient to transfer knowledge learned on LIBs during the last three decades to the development of NIBs.  Following this logic, rapid progress has been made in NIB cathode materials, where layered metal oxides and polyanionic compds. exhibit encouraging results.  On the anode side, pure graphite as the std. anode for LIBs can only form NaC64 in NIBs if solvent co-intercalation does not occur due to the unfavorable thermodn.  In fact, it was the utilization of a carbon anode in LIBs that enabled the com. successes.  Anodes of metal-ion batteries det. key characteristics, such as safety and cycling life; thus, it is indispensable to identify suitable anode materials for NIBs.In this Account, we review recent development on anode materials for NIBs.  Due to the limited space, we will mainly discuss carbon-based and alloy-based anodes and highlight progress made in our groups in this field.  We first present what is known about the failure mechanism of graphite anode in NIBs.  We then go on to discuss studies on hard carbon anodes, alloy-type anodes, and org. anodes.  Esp., the multiple functions of natural cellulose that is used as a low-cost carbon precursor for mass prodn. and as a soft substrate for tin anodes are highlighted.  The strategies of minimizing the surface area of carbon anodes for improving the first-cycle Coulombic efficiency are also outlined, where graphene oxide was employed as dehydration agent and 2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidine-1-oxyl (TEMPO) was used to unzip wood fiber.  Furthermore, surface modification by at. layer deposition technol. is introduced, where we discover that a thin layer of Al2O3 can function to encapsulate Sn nanoparticles, leading to a much enhanced cycling performance.  We also highlight recent work about the phosphorene/graphene anode, which outperformed other anodes in terms of capacity.  The arom. org. anode is also studied as anode with very high initial sodiation capacity.  Furthermore, electrochem. intercalation of Na ions into reduced graphene oxide is applied for fabricating transparent conductors, demonstrating the great feasibility of Na ion intercalation for optical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorKnQvVSK3P7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li59inb0WZAoQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVGksrg%253D&amp;md5=90d2907a2b33fdcf59913255073dd969</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1021/nl303305c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The microstructural changes and phase transformations of tin nanoparticles during electrochem. sodiation were studied with a nanosized sodium ion battery using in situ TEM.  The first sodiation process occurred in two steps; i.e., the cryst. Sn nanoparticles were initially sodiated via a two-phase mechanism with a migrating phase boundary to form a Na-poor, amorphous NaxSn alloy (x ∼ 0.5), which was further sodiated to several Na-rich amorphous phases and finally to the crystd. Na15Sn4 (x = 3.75) via a single-phase mechanism.  The volumetric expansion was ∼60% in the first step and 420% after the second step.  However, despite the huge expansion, cracking or fracture was not obsd., which is attributed to the second step of the single-phase sodiation that accommodates large portion of the sodiation-induced stress over the entire particle.  Excellent cyclability was also obsd. during the reversible sodiation/desodiation cycles, showing great potential of Sn nanoparticles as a robust electrode material for rechargeable batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq4-_RnLjqf7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li1VDT_XvpQWg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsFGqs7rO&amp;md5=3a9b283edadab9ac6dda7ef5c8bd18b7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200346"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Performance of Porous Carbon/Tin Composite Anodes for Sodium-Ion and Lithium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Research on Na-ion batteries is focused on finding suitable electrode materials that enable cell reactions of high energy densities combined with low cost.  Naturally, an assessment of potential electrode materials requires a rational comparison with the analog reaction in Li-ion batteries.  The authors discuss the broad range of different conversion reactions for Na-ion batteries based on their basic thermodn. properties and compare them with their Li analogs.  Capacities, voltages, energy densities and vol. expansions are summarized to sketch out the scope for future studies in this research field.  For a given conversion electrode material, replacing Li by Na leads to a const. shift in cell potential ΔEo(Li-Na) depending on the material class.  For chlorides ΔEo(Li-Na) is nearly zero.  The theor. energy densities of conversion reactions of Na with fluorides or chlorides as pos. electrode materials typically reach values between 700 W h kg-1 and 1000 W h kg-1.  Next to the thermodn. assessment, results on several conversion reactions between Cu compds. (CuS, CuO, CuCl, CuCl2) and Na are being discussed.  Reactions with CuS and CuO were chosen because these compds. are frequently studied for conversion reactions with Li.  Chlorides are interesting because of ΔEo(Li-Na) ≈ 0 V As a result of chloride soly. in the electrolyte, the conversion process proceeds at defined potentials under rather small kinetic limitations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvFdD49Ctj47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljVkTdCpAfUag"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsVWrtbrE&amp;md5=7135136abf30b3a83827d65de85c8f45</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2014.02.002"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yuan, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, M.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Transition Metal Oxides for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Energy</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">5</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">60</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">66</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

To explore high performance anode materials for SIBs is highly desired subject to the current anode research mainly limited to carbonaceous materials.  In this study, a series of transition metal oxides (TMOs) is successfully demonstrated as anodes for SIBs for the first time.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal oxides can be considered as appealing candidates for sodium ion battery anode materials because these low-cost materials possess high capacity and enhanced safety.  However, the practical application of these materials is usually limited by their low electronic cond. and serious vol. change during the charging-discharging process.  Herein, we report the fabrication of 3D-0D graphene-Fe3O4 quantum dot hybrids by a facile one-pot hydrothermal approach as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  Fe3O4 quantum dots with an av. size of 4.9 nm are anchored on the surface of 3D structured graphene nanosheets homogeneously.  Such unique hierarchical structure are advantageous for enlarging the electrode/electrolyte interface area and enhancing the electrochem. activity of the hybrid materials, inhibiting particle aggregation of Fe3O4 and accommodating their vol. change during the charging-discharging process as well as enabling fast diffusion of electrons and rapid transfer of electrolyte ions.  Consequently, the 3D-0D graphene-Fe3O4 quantum dot hybrids exhibit ultrahigh sodium storage capacity (525 mAh/g at 30 mA/g), outstanding cycling stability (312 mAh/g after 200 cycles at 50 mA/g) and superior rate performance (56 mAh/g at 10 A/g).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqySiZNElc5Z7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhx0G1MTbs9ww"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsFanu7rN&amp;md5=18e241446b270b8baeccc2c7fb9c0f0f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1039/c3cc40448j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ahn, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">SnO<sub>2</sub>@Graphene Nanocomposites as Anode Materials for Na-Ion Batteries with Superior Electrochemical Performance</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MnFe2O4@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Zhang, Ning; Yu, Chuanming; Jiao, Lifang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">MnFe2O4 nanodots (∼3.3 nm) homogeneously dispersed in porous nitrogen-doped carbon nanofibers (denoted as MFO@C) were prepd. by a feasible electrospinning technique.  Meanwhile, MFO@C with the character of flexible free-standing membrane was directly used as binder- and current collector-free anode for sodium-ion batteries, exhibiting high electrochem. performance with high-rate capability (305 mA h g-1 at 10000 mA g-1 in comparison of 504 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1) and ultralong cycling life (ca. 90% capacity retention after 4200 cycles).  The Na-storage mechanism was systematically studied, revealing that MnFe2O4 is converted into metallic Mn and Fe after the first discharge (MnFe2O4 + 8Na+ + 8e- → Mn + 2Fe + 4Na2O) and then to MnO and Fe2O3 during the following charge (Mn + 2Fe + 4Na2O → MnO + Fe2O3 + 8Na+ + 8e-).  The subsequent cycles occur through reversible redox reactions of MnO + Fe2O3 + 8Na+ + 8e- &lt;-&gt; Mn + 2Fe + 4Na2O, of which the redn./oxidn. of MnO/Mn takes place at a lower potential than that of Fe2O3/Fe.  Furthermore, a soft package sodium-ion full battery with MFO@C anode and Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C cathode was assembled, delivering a stable capacity of ∼400 mA h g-1 for MFO@C (with 100 cycles at 500 mA g-1) and a promising energy d. of 77.8 Wh kg-1 for the whole battery.  This is owing to the distinctive structure of very-fine MnFe2O4 nanodots embedded in porous N-doped carbon nanofibers, which effectively improves the utilization rate of active materials, facilitates the transportation of electrons and Na+ ions, and prevents the particle pulverization/agglomeration upon prolonged cycling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo_fovS2KMexbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhx0G1MTbs9ww"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XlsFKqtLk%253D&amp;md5=1f0fa2211d961158137c8cc4eb8fdd4c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201601329"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Considering that the high capacity, long-term cycle life, and high-rate capability of anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is a bottleneck currently, a series of Co-doped FeS2 solid solns. with different Co contents were prepd. by a facile solvothermal method, and for the first time their Na-storage properties were investigated.  The optimized Co0.5Fe0.5S2 (Fe0.5) has discharge capacities of 0.220 Ah g-1 after 5000 cycles at 2 A g-1 and 0.172 Ah g-1 even at 20 A g-1 with compatible ether-based electrolyte in a voltage window of 0.8-2.9 V.  The Fe0.5 sample transforms to layered NaxCo0.5Fe0.5S2 by initial activation, and the layered structure is maintained during following cycles.  The redox reactions of NaxCo0.5Fe0.5S2 are dominated by pseudocapacitive behavior, leading to fast Na+ insertion/extn. and durable cycle life.  A Na3V2(PO4)3/Fe0.5 full cell was assembled, delivering an initial capacity of 0.340 Ah g-1.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqARcXONZc757Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhx0G1MTbs9ww"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsFWntbvL&amp;md5=2d6d2579cfa2e3270c7a3b6fee44c500</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201500196"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">FeSe<sub>2</sub> Microspheres as a High-Performance Anode Material for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. The FeSe2 anode also exhibits long-term cyclic stability (372 mA h g-1 with capacity retention of 89.0% after 2000 cycles at 1 A g-1 ) and excellent high-rate performance 388 mA h g-1 at 10 C, 337 mA h g-1 at 20 C, 226 mA h g-1 at 50 C, where 1 C = 0.5 A g-1. Furthermore, the FeSe2 anode is coupled with an excessive Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode (the capacity of the Na3V2(PO4)3 is 1.5-2 times more than that of FeSe2) to fabricate a full cell, which exhibits a discharge capacity of 366 mA h g-1 based on the mass of FeSe2 with an av. output voltage of about 1.7 V at 1 A g-1.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqNZQmIWuA02rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhD7ULbjrFmqQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXmslens7Y%253D&amp;md5=0ecc7e52e3b809d3dadbf828186e0859</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref28" class="rightTabRefNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit28" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201602608"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Park, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Urchin-Like CoSe<sub>2</sub> as a High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Urchin-like CoSe2 assembled by nanorods was synthesized via simple solvothermal route and was 1st applied as an anode material for Na-ion batteries (SIBs) with ether-based electrolytes.  The CoSe2 delivers excellent sodiation and de-sodiation properties when using 1 M NaCF3SO3 in diethyleneglycol dimethylether as an electrolyte and cycling between 0.5 and 3.0 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na-ion batteries have attracted great interest as a low cost and environmentally benign technol. for large scale elec. energy storage, however their development is hindered by the lack of suitable anodic host materials.  In this paper, a green approach is described for the synthesis of Sn4P3/C nanocomposite and demonstrated its excellent Na-storage performance as a novel anode of Na-ion batteries.  This Sn4P3/C anode can deliver a very high reversible capacity of 850 mA h g-1 with a remarkable rate capability with 50% capacity output at 500 mA g-1 and can also be cycled with 86% capacity retention over 150 cycles due to a synergistic Na-storage mechanism in the Sn4P3 anode, where the Sn nanoparticles act as electronic channels to enable electrochem. activation of the P component, while the elemental P and its sodiated product Na3P serve as a host matrix to alleviate the aggregation of the Sn particles during Na insertion reaction.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to the abundance and low cost of sodium-contg. precursors ambient temp. sodium ion batteries are promising for large scale grid storage.  The low m.p. of Na (97.7 °C) compared to 180.6 °C for Li represents a significant safety hazard for the use of Na metal anodes at ambient temps., which emphasizes the need for scientists and engineers to identify, design and develop new neg. electrodes for Na-ion batteries.  The identification of a suitable neg. electrode is a crucial challenge for any further successful development of new cells, and to date efficient and competitive neg. electrodes for NaB are still very rare.  In this work we demonstrate that NiP3 could be a good challenger for this purpose.  NiP3 based electrodes are evaluated as neg. electrode materials for Li-ion batteries (LiB) and Na-ion batteries (NaB).  The study of the reaction mechanism reveals the formation of a phase of compn. close to Li3P and Na3P embedding Ni nanoparticles as the final reaction product after a full discharge.  While the direct conversion of NiP3 into Na3P is identified for the reaction vs. Na, it is still unclear whether an amorphous phase exists during the first discharge for the reaction vs. Li before the conversion.                         rightTab-cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards more sustainable negative electrodes in Na-ion batteries via nanostructured iron oxide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Valvo, M.; Lindgren, F.; Lafont, U.; Bjoerefors, F.; Edstroem, K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion technol. could emerge as an alternative to Li-ion batteries due to limited costs and vast availability of sodium, as well as its similar chem.  Several Na-rich compds. were proposed as pos. electrodes, whereas suitable neg. counterparts were not found yet.  Nanostructured iron oxide is reported here for the 1st time as a potentially viable neg. electrode for Na-ion cells based on conventional electrolytes and composite coatings with CM-cellulose.  Electrochem. reactions of Na+ and Li+ ions with nanostructured Fe2O3 are analyzed and compared.  Initial sodiation of Fe2O3 yields a sloping profile in a voltage range characteristic for oxide conversion, which instead generates a typical plateau upon lithiation.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Relatively high cost and limited resources are limiting the broad applications of Li-ion batteries.  Na-ion batteries are one of the most promising replacement candidates because of abundant sodium resources, low price and similarity with Li insertion chem.  For the first time, γ-Fe2O3 film was grown directly on a copper sheet with radio frequency magnetron sputtering as an anode for Na-ion batteries.  Results show that the γ-Fe2O3 film annealed at 600 °C delivers a high reversible capacity of 450 mA h g-1 and nearly 100% capacity retention over 100 charge-discharge cycles, significantly outperforming all the previously reported examples.  It is believed that the high performance of the Na-ion anode can be attributed to high temp. annealing producing nanopores for a large ion-inserting surface area and uniform γ-Fe2O3 particles firmly loaded on the surface of the Cu substrate to robustly retain the film structure during the Na-ion intercalation process for long cycle life.  This work holds great promise for the fabrication of inexpensive and non-toxic Na-ion batteries for practical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqfEwI227__J7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrG3LMBnJ5Cw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXht1GhurnI&amp;md5=a9169b1bf2e94d1810fe5ef2a0ef51d4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA01334H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhong, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhong, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Z.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Subunits Controlled Synthesis of α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> Multi-Shelled Core–Shell Microspheres and Their Effects on Lithium/Sodium Ion Battery Performances</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Two kinds of Fe2O3 core-shell microspheres were synthesized.  The Fe2O3 sample obtained using ethanol (E-Fe2O3) contains a shell and a core assembled by nanoparticles with a diam. of ∼150 nm and the surface is fairly smooth.  Fe2O3 with optimized subunits was produced using water (W-Fe2O3).  The core is assembled by smaller nanoparticles of ∼50 nm.  The thicker shell and exterior surface possess porous nanorods.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Fe2O3-reduced graphene oxide (RGO) composites were successfully fabricated via a facile microwave-assisted redn. of graphite oxide in Fe2O3 precursor soln. using a microwave system, and studied as anode material for Na ion batteries (SIBs).         </div><div class="casAbstract">α-Fe2O3 nanorod/reduced graphene oxide nanosheet composites (denoted as α-Fe2O3@r-GO NRAs) are fabricated by using a facile and scalable seed-assisted hydrothermal growth route, in which the α-Fe2O3 nanorods are assembled onto the side surfaces of r-GO nanosheets.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of recent developments in nanostructured electrode materials for Li-ion batteries and fuel cell.  A challenge for the society is providing powerful electrochem. energy conversion and storage devices.  Rechargeable Li-ion batteries and fuel cells are promising candidates in terms of energy densities and power densities.  Nanostructured materials are of interest for such devices because of their high surface area, novel size effects and significantly enhanced kinetics.  The benefits of nanometer-size effects, the disadvantages of nanomaterials and strategies to solve these issues such as nano/micro hierarchical structures and surface coatings, as well as developments in the discovery of nanostructured Pt-based electrocatalysts for direct MeOH fuel cells are presented.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We have investigated the properties of the electrode/electrolyte interfaces of composite electrodes based on nanostructured iron oxide cycled in Li- and Na-half cells contg. analogous electrolytes (i.e., LiClO4 or NaClO4 in ethylene carbonate:diethyl carbonate (EC:DEC)).  A meticulous nondestructive step-by-step anal. of the first discharge/charge cycle has been conducted via soft XPS using synchrotron radiation.  In this way, different depths were probed by varying the photon energy (hν) for both electrochem. systems.  The results of this thorough study clearly highlight the differences and the similarities of their resp. solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layers in terms of formation, compn., structure, or thickness, as well as their conversion mechanisms.  We specifically point out that the SEI coverage is more pronounced, and a homogeneous distribution rich in inorg. species exists in the case of Na, compared to the org./inorg. layered structure obsd. for the Li system.  The SEI formation gradually occurs during the first discharge in both Li- and Na-half cells.  For Na, a predeposit layer is formed directly by simple contact of the electrode with the electrolyte.  Despite using similar electrolytes, the nature of the cation (Li+ or Na+) has significant impact on the overall compn./structure of the resulting solid electrolyte interface (SEI).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrRCcAyoMEwYbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0linknUNCn1Ibw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtlyms7nK&amp;md5=901217d73b4847de5186812a880f7095</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.03.151"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tetragonal Hematite Single Crystals as Anode Materials for High Performance Lithium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Understanding the correlation between the desired morphol. of nanostructures and its electrochem. properties is a prerequisite for widespread application of advanced energy materials.  Herein, 2 types of tetragonal α-Fe2O3 single crystals with a mean size of ∼200 nm, including cubic and thorhombic shapes, have been synthesized via a facile hydrothermal approach.  The as-obtained shape of α-Fe2O3 nanocrystals depends on the addn. of the metal ions precursor, the Zn2+ ions result in the cubic shape and the Cu2+ ions result in the thorhombic shape, resp.  These 2 different tetragonal α-Fe2O3 single crystals are used as anode materials for Li ion batteries (LIBs), and cubic α-Fe2O3 single crystal exhibits a better performance than thorhombic α-Fe2O3 single crystal.  The discharge capacity of cubic α-Fe2O3 single crystal is up to 1028 mA-h/g, and the c.d. is up to 1000 mA/g (1C) after 222 cycles.  Clearly, the α-Fe2O3 single crystal with controlled shapes would improve the electrochem. performance of LIBs as superior anode materials, and this approach could pave a way to develop high performance LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrKslu_LxPjzbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0linknUNCn1Ibw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXltlKmur0%253D&amp;md5=a6ce93ab309330b8a59f5b4656063771</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1038/srep05753"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xie, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">CuO Single Crystal with Exposed {001} Facets-A Highly Efficient Material for Gas Sensing and Li-Ion Battery Applications</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Sci.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Single-cryst. bilayered V oxide nanobelts were synthesized by a simple solvothermal method.  FE-SEM and AFM analyses identified the nanobelt morphol. of the as-prepd. V oxide with a rectangular cross-section and a thickness of ∼50 nm.  XRD and TEM characterizations revealed a large (001) interlayer spacing (∼11.53 Å), which can accommodate Na-ion insertion and extn.  When applied as cathode materials in Na-ion batteries, V oxide nanobelts exhibited a high capacity of 231.4 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 80 mA g-1. These superior electrochem. performances could be ascribed to the unique bilayered V oxide nanobelts with dominantly exposed {100} crystal planes, which provide large interlayer spacing for facile Na-ion insertion/extn.  Single-cryst. bilayered V oxide nanobelts could be promising cathode materials for high-performance Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr9Zt4QF5KMurVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhPVPDdp6wQ7w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslGgsLjF&amp;md5=0a89c6f143902090fa42787be2f1e390</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1039/c4ta01751j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hierarchical Orthorhombic V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> Hollow Nanospheres as High Performance Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.
